[2026-02-17T04:23:58Z] Saumay: With answers: ↵ This saga begins with King Z [Charles V], a 16th-century colossus who held the ultimate "Iron Throne" of his era. His dominion was so vast that the phrase "the sun never sets" was first coined for his empire. Though he was the King of A [Spain], he also reigned as the B [Holy Roman] Emperor. Exhausted by the weight of these crowns, he shocked the world by abdicating, enacting a "Grand Split" that divided his house into A [Spanish] and C [Austrian] branches. ↵ The A[Spanish] Branch established its seat in Capital D [Madrid], ruling a global empire of gold and galleons. However, this line withered in 1700, sparking a war that engulfed the world as rival powers fought to claim the vacant throne. Meanwhile, the C[Habsburg] Branch made its home in Capital E [Vienna], building a glittering theater of power that would dictate the culture of the region for centuries. ↵ The family’s survival was tested in the 18th century by Empress Y [Maria Theresa], the dynasty’s only female sovereign. She survived a desperate war of succession and bore 16 children, using them as political glue for the empire. To showcase her global reach, she curated the ‘Millions Room’ in her summer palace at Capital E [Schönbrunn Palace, Vienna], decorating it with rare rosewood and miniatures from the F [Mughal] courts of India, featuring portraits of the emperor X [Shah Jahan]. ↵ In a desperate bid to end three centuries of bloodshed, she orchestrated the ultimate "Diplomatic Revolution" by marrying her daughter, W [Marie Antoinette], to King V [Louis XVI], the ruler of the House of G [Bourbon], the dynasty’s most bitter and ancestral rival. However, this union ended not in peace, but at the foot of the guillotine during the H [French] Revolution. ↵ Out of that revolutionary smoke rose U [Napoleon Bonaparte], a usurper who shattered the old world order. He proved so formidable that T [Francis II], the grandson of Y[Maria Theresa], made a desperate move to protect the dignity of his ancestors. In 1806, T[Francis II] chose to officially dissolve the B [Holy Roman] Empire, permanently extinguishing a crown that had endured for a thousand years. By killing the title himself, he ensured the conqueror could never seize the mantle for his own. To secure a fragile peace, [Francis II] was eventually forced to offer his own daughter [Marie Louise] as a bride to U[Napoleon]. It was a union the usurper craved, for despite his genius, he was an upstart who hungered for the prestige of the C [Habsburg] Dynasty’s ancient "Blue Blood" to legitimize his new regime. ↵ By the mid-19th century, the family faced a new rival, S [Otto von Bismarck], hailing from I [Prussia]. He provoked a lightning-fast war that humiliated the dynasty, excluding them from their ancestral lands forever. Out of this victory, S forged a powerful new empire, J [German Empire], which rose to challenge the old order. Forced to look East to save his crumbling power, Emperor R [Franz Joseph I] reinvented his realm through a historic compromise, transforming his domain into a ‘Dual’ Monarchy that shared power between Capital E [Vienna] and the rising Capital K [Budapest], a move that elevated the Land L [Hungary] to equal status with his own. To project a new image of strength, he celebrated what history remembers as the ‘Wedding of the 19th Century’ to his rebellious, beautiful cousin, P [Elisabeth "Sisi"]. ↵ The dynasty’s long sunset ended with Q [Archduke Franz Ferdinand], the nephew of Emperor Franz Joseph 1, in the early 20th century. Because the Emperor’s only son [Rudolf] had perished in a tragic scandal, the weight of the 600-year-old dynasty fell on Q. What was intended as a routine display of imperial presence in a restive corner of the realm [Sarajevo] became the final curtain call for the age of kings. ‎<This message was edited>

[2026-02-18T01:41:25Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: FUQ.	While betting on Jesus coming back might seem like a novelty, the economic theory behind markets like Polymarket is widely used to seriously forecast election results, box office returns, and even flu outbreaks. The central premise is that the aggregated average of independent estimates is often more accurate than any single expert's opinion. This phenomenon was famously observed by Victorian statistician Francis Galton in 1906, when he noticed that 800 fairgoers—a mix of experts and amateurs—collectively guessed the weight of a slaughtered X to within 1% of its actual weight. Y, a 2004 book by James Surowiecki, popularized this concept. ID X and Y.

By providing a mathematical skeleton for the invisible structures observed by his predecessor, E resolved the nature of a phenomenon that had remained a mystery for millennia by mathematically predicting the existence of Z. ↵ The physical proof of this theory arrived nearly a decade after E's passing, when G validated the existence of Z within his laboratory. Although his career was cut tragically short, his name was immortalized as the global standard used in the measurement of the very phenomenon he confirmed. ↵ These discoveries were so vital that F, who was famously born in the very same year that E passed away, kept portraits of B, D, and E on his study wall as a silent tribute to the men whose work he eventually sought to unify. ‎<This message was edited>

[2026-02-18T07:06:44Z] Saumay: Answer: ↵ The evolution of modern physics is marked by a beautiful intellectual inheritance. The saga begins with Robert Hooke, a master of many sciences and the original Curator of The Royal Society, whose legacy was famously obscured by his successor. That successor was Isaac Newton, a foundational giant who stewarded The Royal Society for over two decades; clearly not fond of his predecessor, he allegedly ensured that Hooke's portrait was lost to history. ↵ A century later, Humphry Davy occupied that same presidential chair, but his legacy is most famously tied to his role as a mentor. He famously mentioned that his "greatest discovery" was actually Michael Faraday, though professional friction later clouded their relationship and led Davy to oppose his protégé’s rise. ↵ This protégé, Michael Faraday, an uneducated bookbinder’s apprentice, rose to become a master experimentalist who fundamentally changed our understanding of space. He envisioned "lines of force" filling the vacuum, yet he humbly declined the presidency of The Royal Society that his predecessors had so closely guarded. ↵ The transition from physical intuition to formal theory was completed by James Clerk Maxwell, who acted as the intellectual successor to Faraday's visual legacy. By providing a mathematical skeleton for the invisible structures observed by his predecessor, Maxwell resolved the nature of a phenomenon that had remained a mystery for millennia by mathematically predicting the existence of Electromagnetic Waves. ↵ The physical proof of this theory arrived nearly a decade after Maxwell's passing, when Heinrich Hertz validated the existence of Electromagnetic Waves within his laboratory. Although his career was cut tragically short, his name was immortalized as the global standard used in the measurement of the very phenomenon he confirmed. ↵ These discoveries were so vital that Albert Einstein, who was famously born in the very same year that Maxwell passed away, kept portraits of Newton, Faraday, and Maxwell on his study wall as a silent tribute to the men whose work he eventually sought to unify. ‎<This message was edited>

[2026-02-18T21:26:03Z] Smit D Mahajani: I personally think that it's simply due to both being products of their times. The Concert of Europe was during the colonial era, where balance of power and spheres of influence were the primary prisms through which the European powers looked at the world. This lead to a cynical solution rooted in preventing violence by threat of more violence ↵ In contrast, the EU project arose out of a frustration caused by a cyclical pattern of violence and conflict going back many decades if not centuries in Europe. And the world was also not one dominated by a colonial mindset. So the focus shifted to healthy cooperation and not cynicism ‎<This message was edited>
[2026-02-18T21:27:13Z] Smit D Mahajani: Exactly. Very well said
